Chapter 1

Chapter 1

Father’s will arrived before his body did. My inheritance, the solicitor’s letter explained, was conditional: I must reside at the family seat, Greymarsh, for one full year. Mother’s silence in the adjoining room was a verdict already passed.



I observed it from my perch on the ottoman, a volume of Tennyson open but unread upon my lap. The post had been laid upon the salver by Mrs. Briggs, whose face was a study in carefully arranged neutrality. My father, behind the fortress of The Times, remained a rustling expanse of broadsheet. My mother’s inhalation was short, tight, a stitch pulled too fast in the tapestry of our afternoon quiet.

“It is from your Aunt Maud,” she said, not to anyone in particular. Her voice was the peculiar, flat tone she reserved for announcements of minor calamities—a broken Spode platter, a spot of rain on a planned picnic.

My father’s paper did not lower. “Hmm.”

“From Florence.”

The newspaper descended by a precise inch. Two grey eyes, keen as flint, appeared over the top. “Indeed.”

The air in the drawing-room, always thick with the smell of beeswax, lavender polish, and the faint, perpetual ghost of mutton, seemed to grow denser. Aunt Maud was not spoken of. She existed in our family lexicon as a pronoun, a vague “she” or “her,” referenced in the past tense, as one might speak of a distant, slightly disreputable ancestor. She was my father’s elder sister, who twenty years prior had done the unthinkable: following the death of her husband, the respectable but dull Colonel Archibald Finch, she had not retired to Cheltenham to make a shrine of her grief. She had taken her modest inheritance and fled to Italy. To Florence. She painted. She wrote letters to newspapers about the plight of the contadini. She was, in my mother’s hushed parlance, “unmoored.”

Mother slit the envelope with a jade-handled paperknife, its movement unnaturally slow. As she read, a faint, vertical line appeared between her brows, the one she called her “thinking line” but which we all knew was the groove worn by disapproval. I watched her eyes, a pale, clear blue, dart back and forth. Her lips, usually set in a firm, rosebud line, parted slightly.

“Oh,” she breathed. The syllable was a dropped stitch, a small hole in the fabric of her composure.

Father lowered the paper fully now, folding it with crisp, military precision—a habit from his days in the Judge Advocate General’s corps. “Out with it, Eleanor. What does the woman want?”

“It is not what she wants, Charles. It is what she proposes.” Mother looked up, her gaze sweeping past my father and landing on me with a force that made my spine straighten. “She proposes that Helena should go to her. For the season. The spring season in Florence.”

The silence that followed was absolute, broken only by the greedy crackle of the coal in the grate. I felt the blood drain from my face, then flood back in a hot, confused tide. Florence. The word itself was like a colour from a forbidden paintbox—sienna, ochre, gold. Not the polite, watercolour washes of our London life.

“Preposterous,” my father said, but it was the automatic response of a man loading a familiar cannon. He was looking at me, assessing. I was nineteen. The London season two years prior had been a qualified, dismal failure. I was too tall, my opinions too readily formed, my conversation inclined towards books and botany rather than the acceptable trifles. I had been labelled “clever,” which, in the marriage market, was a polite synonym for “difficult.” Two subsequent, quieter seasons had yielded nothing but a deepening sense of quiet panic within my mother’s breast and a settled, grey resignation within my own.

“She writes most persuasively,” Mother continued, her voice regaining some of its managerial timbre. She was reading ahead, searching for ammunition. “She speaks of the galleries, the improving society of expatriates, the climate for Helena’s chest…” I had no ailment of the chest, but it was a useful, unobjectionable fiction. “She has a spacious apartment on the Lungarno. A companion, a Miss…Swift, is mentioned. A clergyman’s daughter, thoroughly suitable. It would be,” and here she met my father’s gaze squarely, “an education. Of a sort.”

“An education in Bohemianism and impropriety,” Father grumbled, but the fight was leaving him. I could see the calculations proceeding behind his eyes. I was an expense. A lingering problem. Aunt Maud’s offer was a solution, neatly packaged and requiring no expenditure from his own pocket. It was also a form of exile, which held a certain appeal. A difficult daughter, shipped to a difficult sister. There was a poetic economy to it.

“May I see the letter, Mother?” I asked, my voice surprisingly steady.

She hesitated, then passed the single sheet across the space between us. The paper was fine, creamy, and smelled faintly of citrus and something else—oil paint, perhaps. The handwriting was not the elegant, sloping copperplate of English ladies, but a bold, black, angular script, dashes and dots like flung ink.

My dear Eleanor, it began, bypassing my father entirely.

You will no doubt be surprised to hear from me. Time, as they say, marches, though I find it here in Florence more inclined to a leisurely stroll. The purpose of my writing is your daughter, Helena. I have seen no likeness since she was a child, a solemn little thing with eyes too big for her face, but one hears things. One hears she has a mind, which in a young woman is so often a burden to be borne quietly or, worse, beaten into submission…

I caught my breath. No one, not even my most progressive governess, had ever put it so baldly.

…I propose to relieve you of this burden for a time. Send her to me for the spring. I will not promise to make her a marriageable commodity—I have no skill in that market. But I can promise her light, and colour, and a world larger than the view from a drawing-room window. I can show her that a woman’s life need not be a single, pre-bound volume, but a library of possibilities. Consider it. There is a boat train from Charing Cross every Thursday.

Yours, with more affection than you likely credit,
Maud.

I looked up. The words light, and colour, and a world larger were beating a tattoo against my ribs. My father was watching me.

“Well, Helena? The idea appals you, I trust? All that garlic and papist statuary.”

I knew my role. I was to be reluctant, obedient, to yield to their decision. But the letter in my hand was a key, warm from the lock it had just turned. I chose my words with the care of a sapper laying a charge.

“It sounds…educational, Papa. As Mama says. And if Aunt Maud has a suitable companion…it would not be…unseemly.”

My mother pounced on this. “Exactly, Charles. It is not as if she would be unchaperoned. And Maud is family, however eccentric. It might give Helena…polish. A certain conversational breadth she currently lacks.”

“Conversational breadth about frescoes and olive oil,” he muttered. But he sighed, the long, weary exhalation of a man conceding a minor, messy battlefield. “Write back. Get particulars. The exact situation of this companion. References, if such a person can provide them. The names of respectable families in her circle. We are not sending her into a den of artists and radicals without assurances.”

It was settled. Just like that. The course of my life, which had seemed a narrow, straight gravel path vanishing into a grey fog, had forked violently. One prong remained here, in this drawing-room, with its Turkey carpet and its predictable, slow descent into spinsterhood and good works. The other led to a city of sun and stone, and a woman who wrote letters that smelled of paint and rebellion.

The next fortnight was a whirlwind of practicalities conducted in a strange, tense atmosphere. Trunks were hauled from the attic. My wardrobe, all muted fawns and dove greys, was examined and found wanting. “You cannot wear half-mourning in the Italian spring, Helena,” my mother fretted, as if bright colours were a moral hazard. A new dress was commissioned, a “walking dress” in a deep, forest green that made my skin look less pale. It felt like a costume for a part I did not yet know how to play.

My mother’s conversations with me took on a new, urgent tone. They were less instructions than inoculations.

“Your aunt’s opinions are her own, Helena. You are not required to adopt them. Consider them…curiosities.”
“A gentleman is a gentleman, whether he is English or Italian. Do not be dazzled by foreign manners.”
“Write every week. Not those fanciful descriptions, but the facts. Who you meet, where you go. The state of your health.”
“And for heaven’s sake, do not let her paint you. It is not dignified.”

On the eve of my departure, my father summoned me to his study. The room was his sanctum: leather, law reports, the faint, sweet smell of pipe tobacco and port. He stood by the fireplace, one elbow on the mantel, a pose I knew from his rare, solemn addresses.

“Helena,” he began. “This Italian jaunt. Your mother sees it as a finishing school. I see it as a strategic retreat. You have not…taken, here. This may provide a different…theatre.” He cleared his throat, uncomfortable with metaphor. “Your aunt is a woman of strong, and to my mind, erroneous, convictions. You are of an age where you might be susceptible to such things. Remember who you are. You are a Verney of Suffolk. Our name may not be in Debrett’s, but it stands for something. For duty. For respectability. For the quiet, unshowy maintenance of order. Do not be seduced by…vividness.”

He handed me a sealed envelope. “A letter of credit. Be prudent. And this.” It was a small, leather-bound Bible. “A guide more reliable than any Baedeker.”

I took them, the cool leather of the Bible, the crisp paper of the credit. “Thank you, Papa.”

He nodded, his duty discharged. “We shall expect you home by June. The London season will be underway. The…experience…may have given you some useful gloss.”

That night, in my room, the trunks stood like sentinels by the door. I opened the Bible. A slip of paper fell out, in my father’s precise hand. A list of names, headed British Consul, Florence. Respectable Contacts. I closed the book. From my window, I could see the yellow fog coiling around the gas lamps of Queen’s Gate. Tomorrow, it would be the Channel, then the long, unspooling ribbon of railway through France, and then…Italy. Aunt Maud. A library of possibilities.

I felt not excitement, but a profound, trembling terror. As if I had been standing safely on the shore all my life, and had just been handed the plans for a ship.

---

Chapter 2

Chapter 2

The journey was a prolonged, rattling dislocation. The world softened, then sharpened, then burst into a riot of colour my eyes struggled to believe. The grim, wet greens of Normandy gave way to the hard, brilliant light of the Midi, and finally, after a night in a Marseilles hotel that smelled of garlic and drains, to the slow, climbing train into the Apennines. My travelling companion, a Miss Pettigrew returning to her position as a governess in Turin, had talked incessantly of moral fibre and the perils of garlic until we parted ways at Genoa. Her place was taken by a voluble Italian matron with a basket of lemons, who smiled at my halting French and offered me a fruit, its scent so pungent and alive it seemed to cut through the fug of coal smoke and anxiety.

I arrived in Florence as the afternoon sun was gilding the city, turning the river to molten copper. Firenze. The name was a whisper on the lips of the porters. I stood on the platform at Santa Maria Novella, clutching my valise, my trunk being wrestled somewhere behind me, feeling utterly, desolately adrift. The air was cool but soft, carrying smells of coffee, dust, and a faint, sour note of the river.

“Miss Verney?”
The voice was English, crisp, and devoid of warmth. I turned to see a woman of perhaps thirty-five, dressed in a severely cut dress of navy blue, her hair tucked under a plain bonnet. Her face was long, intelligent, and unsmiling. This was Miss Swift, the clergyman’s daughter, the “thoroughly suitable” companion.

“I am. And you are Miss Swift?”
“I am. Your aunt sent the carriage. It is this way.” She turned and began walking, not checking to see if I followed. I scurried after her, my London boots slipping on the smooth flagstones.

The carriage was a small, open calesse. As we clattered through the bustling, noisy streets, I tried to take it in—the towering, severe facades of the palaces, the sudden, breathtaking glimpses of a dome or a campanile, the throngs of people who seemed to talk with their whole bodies. It was a symphony of stone and sound. Miss Swift sat bolt upright, her hands folded in her lap, a living monument to propriety.

“Your aunt is at home,” she said, as if reporting a meteorological fact. “She was engaged with her painting this morning. She expects you for tea.”

“Thank you. It is all so…vivid.”
“It is foreign,” she corrected. “One adjusts. The English colony here is small but select. We maintain standards.”

We turned onto a broad avenue running alongside the river. The water was green, flowing swiftly under ancient bridges. We stopped before a tall, ochre-washed building. Miss Swift alighted with efficiency. “The apartment is on the third floor. There is no lift.”

The staircase was wide, of worn marble, cool and shadowy. By the time we reached the third landing, my breath was short. Miss Swift produced a key and opened a heavy, dark door.

We stepped into light.

It flooded from tall windows that opened onto a small, wrought-iron balcony overlooking the river. The room was large, sparsely but elegantly furnished. A few good pieces of Tuscan furniture—a carved chest, a sturdy table—stood on terracotta tiles partially covered by a faded, beautiful rug. But it was the walls that commanded attention. They were not papered or painted a polite colour. They were covered, every inch not occupied by window or door, with paintings. Not framed and hung, but pinned, tacked, leaning. Watercolours of the hills, bold oil sketches of faces, quick studies of a bowl of oranges, a twist of cypress trees against a sky. The air smelled of turpentine, linseed oil, and, underlying it, the citrus scent from the letter.

“Helena.”
A woman emerged from an adjoining room. She was not at all as I had pictured. I had imagined someone wild-haired, draped in garish shawls, a female pirate. Aunt Maud was tall, like my father, but where he was solid, she was angular. She wore a simple, paint-stained smock over a grey dress. Her hair, more silver than grey, was swept into a loose, practical knot. Her face was deeply lined, not with the fretful lines of my mother, but with lines of concentration and sun. Her eyes were my father’s flint-grey, but where his were guarded, hers were alight with a fierce, open curiosity. She looked me up and down, not with judgment, but with the keen appraisal of an artist studying a new subject.

“So,” she said, her voice a low, warm contralto. “The solemn little thing. You have your father’s bones. Thank God you have not his expression.” She came forward and, to my shock, took my face gently between her hands. Her fingers were dry, strong, stained with ultramarine and ochre. She turned my head slightly towards the light from the window. “Good. There is something behind the eyes. Not entirely smothered yet. Welcome to Florence, child. You are just in time for the primavera. Miss Swift, tea, if you please. In the loggia.”

Miss Swift, who had been observing this greeting with a pinched expression, gave a tight nod and vanished.

Aunt Maud released me. “You are overwhelmed. It is to be expected. Come.” She led me through the riotous studio to a smaller, glassed-in porch—the loggia—filled with potted lemon trees and wicker chairs. The view was a panoramic sweep of the river, the Ponte Vecchio with its jeweller’s shops clinging like barnacles, and the rolling, green-brown hills beyond.

“Sit. Breathe. Look.” She settled into a chair, pulling a tin of cigarettes from her smock pocket. “Do you mind? A dreadful habit I picked up from the French. Your mother would swoon.”

“I don’t mind,” I whispered, still trying to reconcile this vibrant, smoking creature with the ghostly “she” of our family parlour.

Miss Swift brought tea—a proper English service, but the cups were chipped, the pot not silver but sturdy earthenware. The biscuits were cantucci, hard almond things meant for dipping in wine.

“Now,” Aunt Maud said, exhaling a plume of smoke that curled into the sunlight. “You will have questions. Your parents certainly will. The answers are: Miss Swift is a dragon, but a necessary one. She keeps the tradesmen from cheating me too outrageously and ensures I do not starve when I am working. She is paid quarterly from my funds. This apartment is rented from a contessa who prefers my money to her empty rooms. My circle is mixed—a few English, some Americans (they are refreshingly unencumbered), several Italian gentlemen of the old families who find an eccentric English widow amusing, and, yes, artists. Some talented, some dreadful. We dine at eight. We do not ‘pay calls’ in the English fashion unless I am forced to. You are free to explore the city, but you will take Miss Swift or myself until you know your way. You will learn some Italian. You will, I hope, tell me what you truly think of things, and not what you believe I wish to hear. Is that acceptable?”

It was a torrent. I could only nod. “Yes, Aunt.”

“Good. Your room is through there.” She gestured with her cigarette. “It was the contessa’s sewing room. It has a window with a view of a wall, but the light is good in the morning. Unpack. Rest. We will talk more at dinner.”

My room was small, whitewashed, simple. The promised view was indeed of a sun-bleached ochre wall, but a shaft of late afternoon sun fell across the narrow bed. As I began to unpack my carefully folded, muted English clothes, they looked absurd against the vivid simplicity of the room, like flowers pressed too long in a book. I hung a grey dress in the armoire and it seemed to sigh.

Dinner was a revelation. Served by a silent, elderly Italian woman named Giovanna, it consisted of a soup thick with beans and herbs, a roast chicken fragrant with rosemary and garlic, and a salad of bitter leaves dressed with oil and lemon. The wine was a rough, red Chianti. My aunt drank it like water. Miss Swift sipped hers with disapproval.

“Tomorrow,” Aunt Maud announced over cheese and pears, “we shall begin. We will go to the Uffizi. One must start with the old masters to understand what the modern ones are rebelling against. Or embracing. It is a conversation across centuries, you see.”

“Will Miss Swift accompany us?” I asked.

“Miss Swift finds galleries damp and the nude figures indecent. She will attend to the household accounts. We shall go alone.”

Miss Swift’s knife clinked sharply against her plate. “I should not like Miss Verney to be thought forward, Maud. An unchaperoned girl, even with her aunt…”

“I am fifty-seven years old, Swift. I believe I constitute a chaperone of formidable proportions. Besides, in Florence, one looks at art. It is the primary occupation. It is considered more respectable than gossip, which is London’s chief industry.”

The meal concluded. Miss Swift retired with a headache. Aunt Maud lit another cigarette and poured herself a glass of amber liquid from a decanter. “Grappa,” she said. “It aids digestion. Would you like to see what I am working on?”

I followed her back into the studio. On an easel by the largest window stood a canvas. It was a view from the loggia, but not as it was. The river was a swirl of molten gold and deep green. The buildings on the opposite bank were not solid stone, but suggestions of form, blocks of umber and sienna. The sky was not blue, but a tumultuous mixture of violet and rose and a strange, acidic yellow. It was beautiful and unsettling. It felt more true to the emotion of the scene than a precise rendering would have been.

“It is not finished,” she said, standing beside me. “It may never be. The light changes. The feeling changes. That is the struggle. To pin down a moment before it escapes.” She looked at me. “What do you see?”

I was accustomed to being asked what things were, not what I saw. I hesitated, searching for the correct, art-historical answer. But her gaze demanded honesty. “I see…energy. A kind of…peaceful tumult.”

She stared at me for a long moment, then a slow, genuine smile transformed her face. “Peaceful tumult. Yes. That is exactly it. You have an eye, Helena. Or a heart. They are often the same organ.”

She turned back to the canvas. “Your father thinks I am a frivolous woman playing at a frivolous trade. He is wrong. This,” she said, gesturing at the paint-stained room, the chaotic walls, the glowing canvas, “this is the most serious thing I have ever done. It is my confession. My argument. My prayer. All in one.” She looked at me, her flinty eyes soft. “We shall see what you make of yours.”

That night, in my narrow bed, the sounds of the foreign city drifting up—distant laughter, a guitar, the murmur of the river—I felt the careful scaffolding of my old self begin to tremble. I was no longer just Helena Verney, difficult daughter. I was a girl with an eye, in a room that smelled of paint, in a city of light. And I was terrified, and thrilled, in equal, peaceful tumult.

---

Chapter 3

Chapter 3

The Uffizi was not a gallery; it was a universe, ordered and overwhelming. We walked the long, echoing corridors, under ceilings frothing with gilt and fresco, past ranks of Madonnas and martyrs, Venuses and Virtues. Aunt Maud did not lecture. She would stop before a painting, sometimes for minutes, saying nothing. Then she might murmur a phrase. “Look at the tenderness in that hand.” Or, “See how the red of the robe pulls your eye through the composition.” Or, more bluntly, “A competent piece of propaganda. The Medici paid well.”

I was drowning in beauty, a pleasant, exhausting drowning. My feet ached on the marble. My neck was stiff from looking up. We turned a corner into a smaller room, and she stopped before a painting.

“Here,” she said softly. “This is where we sit.”

It was Botticelli’s Primavera. I had seen engravings, pale ghosts of the original. Nothing prepared me for the reality. It was a meadow in a dream. The figures seemed to float on a tapestry of flowers, their faces pale and poignant, filled with a sweet, melancholy grace. The colours were luminous, delicate yet intense. The figure of Spring, scattering her flowers, had a face of such ethereal beauty it made my heart ache.

“What do you feel?” Aunt Maud asked, her voice low.

I could not find words for the strange, wistful joy it evoked. “It’s…sad. And glorious. All at once.”

“Yes. It is about the fleetingness of beauty. The perfection of a moment just before it dissolves. That is the primavera. Not just spring, but the first truth, the first green. It doesn’t last.” She was silent for a long time. “It is the opposite of what I try to do. I try to capture the energy, the force. Botticelli captures the ghost of it. Perhaps he was wiser.”

We sat on a bench, and I let the painting wash over me. For the first time since my arrival, the frantic buzz of newness quieted. This was a different kind of language, one that spoke directly to a part of me I had no name for.

Our reverie was broken by a voice.
“Maud! I thought it was you. Contemplating the ineffable, as usual?”

A man approached. He was perhaps forty, dressed with a casual elegance—a well-cut but slightly shabby linen suit, no cravat, his dark hair too long. He was handsome in a worn, intelligent way, with a mobile mouth and eyes that missed nothing.

“Leo,” Aunt Maud said, not rising, but her tone warmed. “Helena, this is Mr. Leo Ashton. A fellow exile, though of a more recent vintage. Leo, my niece, Miss Helena Verney, newly arrived from London.”

He took my hand, not shaking it, but holding it for a brief moment. His own were long-fingered, stained with ink. “A refugee from the fog. Welcome. How do you find our pagan paradise?”

“It is…very bright,” I said, retrieving my hand, flustered by his direct gaze.

“It is that. Maud, have you seen the new Cézanne that the Berenson set are all in a froth about? It’s in the Strozzi. A few apples that look like they’ve been carved from granite. Revolutionary, apparently.”

“I have no time for revolutions in still lifes,” Aunt Maud said, but she was smiling. “I am still fighting my own battles with light.”

“Aren’t we all.” His eyes flicked back to me. “And are you an artist, Miss Verney?”

“No. I mean, I sketch a little. Flowers. That sort of thing.”
“The ‘that sort of thing’ is the foundation of everything. Maud, you must bring her to the Villa Rossa on Thursday. The Contessa is having one of her evenings. Music, bad poetry, excellent wine. It will be an education for Miss Verney in how the other half—the artistic half—lives.”

Aunt Maud considered. “Perhaps. If Miss Swift’s headache persists.”
“Do not let the dragon deter you. It is settled.” He gave a slight, theatrical bow. “A pleasure, Miss Verney. Until Thursday.” He sauntered off, leaving a faint scent of tobacco and verbena in his wake.

“Who is he?” I asked as we walked back towards the apartment, the spring sun now high and hot.

“Leo? A writer. Or a talker who sometimes writes. Brilliant, in his way. He published a novel ten years ago that caused a small scandal—too psychological, not enough plot. He lives on pieces for the reviews and the generosity of patrons like the Contessa Rossa. He is charming, unreliable, and has broken the hearts of several silly English girls who mistake his attention for something more permanent. You are not a silly girl, are you, Helena?”

“I hope not, Aunt.”
“Good. Keep hoping.”

The promised invitation to the Villa Rossa arrived the next morning, a thick, cream card embossed with a crest. Miss Swift sniffed as she handed it to my aunt. “The Contessa Rossa. An Italian divorcee. Her salon is notorious.”

“Notorious for good conversation and poor singing, Swift. A fair trade.” Aunt Maud looked at me. “We shall go. You will need the dark green dress. It is the closest thing you have to not looking like a governess.”

The Villa Rossa was a Renaissance palace on the south side of the river, its gardens climbing the hillside. The evening was warm. The green dress did feel different—less like a uniform. My aunt wore a simple gown of deep violet silk, no jewels. We were shown into a vast, frescoed salon where perhaps thirty people were gathered. The air hummed with conversation in Italian, English, French. The women were dressed with a dramatic flair unknown in London; the men were a mix of formal evening wear and artistic negligence. Leo Ashton, in a velvet jacket, detached himself from a group and came to greet us.

“You came! Maud, you look magnificent. Like a thundercloud at sunset. Miss Verney, you have survived your first week. Allow me to introduce you to the fauna.”

He guided us through the crowd. I was introduced to a German sculptor with huge, plaster-dusted hands; an American heiress who was “studying colour theory”; a elderly Italian marchese who kissed my hand and called me bella inglese; and a fierce-looking Frenchwoman who was, Leo whispered, writing a history of women in art.

I was handed a glass of prosecco. The bubbles went straight to my head. The conversation was a whirlwind of ideas—arguments about Ibsen, debates on the merits of the Pre-Raphaelites versus the Impressionists, gossip about who was having an affair with whom in the English colony. I said little, listening, trying to absorb it all. It was intoxicating. This was the “library of possibilities” my aunt had written of. Here, people lived in volumes of their own choosing.

“And what does the new arrival make of us all?” The voice was female, cool, and slightly amused. I turned to see a woman perhaps a few years older than me, strikingly beautiful in a sharp, feline way. She had dark hair piled high, and wore a dress of crimson that would have caused apoplexy in Queen’s Gate. She held a cigarette in a long holder.

“Caterina,” Leo said, a note of caution in his voice. “This is Maud’s niece, Helena. Helena, the Contessa Rossa, our hostess.”

“So you are the mysterious niece,” the Contessa said, her dark eyes appraising me. “Maud has spoken of you. She said you had a quiet eye. I find quiet eyes often see the most.” Her English was flawless, accented. “Are you enjoying our little menagerie?”

“It is very…stimulating,” I managed.
“That is one word for it. Leo, be a darling and fetch me some more of that dreadful champagne. I wish to talk to this quiet girl.”

Leo, with a raised eyebrow at me, obeyed. The Contessa guided me towards a slightly quieter alcove. “You are not like the other English girls who come here. They are either terrified or they become giddy and foolish. You are…observing. That is good. Tell me, what do you think of your aunt’s work?”

The question was direct, like a thrust. “I think it is powerful. It feels…true.”
“True to what?”
“To the feeling of a place. Not just its appearance.”
She smiled, a slow, cat-like smile. “Yes. Maud has passion. It is a rare thing in a woman of her age and class. She has paid for it, of course. Her family, they have erased her, no?”

I flushed. “They do not speak of her often.”
“No. They would not. To live for oneself is the great sin. Especially for a woman.” She took a long drag on her cigarette. “And you? Will you live for yourself, Helena Verney? Or will you let them bind you back into that little book?”

I had no answer. The question was too vast, too terrifying.
“Do not answer now. But think on it. Florence has a way of asking that question, over and over.” She looked over my shoulder. “Ah. The English pastor approaches. My cue to depart. Enjoy your evening, cara.”

She glided away just as a stout, red-faced man in clerical black bore down on me. It was the Reverend Dr. Fanshawe, the chaplain of the English church. I had been pointedly warned to seek him out by my father’s list.

“Miss Verney! I had heard you were in town. With your…aunt.” His jollity seemed forced. “I trust you are finding spiritual sustenance amidst all this…art?”

“The city is very beautiful,” I said carefully.
“Yes, yes. Pagan beauty, of course. One must be vigilant. I understand you were at the Uffizi. A mixed blessing, those paintings. So many naked pagans. I hope your aunt is also ensuring you attend services?”

At that moment, a burst of laughter came from the centre of the room. Leo Ashton was now at the piano, playing a risqué French chanson, and the American heiress was attempting to sing along. The Reverend Fanshawe’s face tightened. “You see the company that is kept. I would be remiss in my duty if I did not advise you, my dear, to be cautious. Your aunt is…well, her reputation is her own. But yours is yet to be formed. Florence can be a perilous place for a young woman’s character.”

His words were a bucket of cold water. The bubbles of prosecco and conversation seemed to pop, leaving the familiar, cold dread of disapproval. He was my father’s voice, here in this glittering, sinful room.

“Thank you for your concern, Reverend,” I said, my voice flat.
“I shall call on you next week. We can have a proper talk. Your father would expect it.”

He moved away. I stood alone in the alcove, the contessa’s question and the reverend’s warning clashing in my head. I looked across the room. My aunt was deep in conversation with the German sculptor, her hands moving, her face alive. Leo caught my eye from the piano and winked.

I felt a sudden, sharp fracture within me. I was not of this world, not truly. But I was no longer of the other one, either. I was suspended between them, a ghost in a green dress, belonging nowhere. The first, green truth of my primavera was this: freedom was not a gift. It was a confrontation. And it was just beginning.



---

Chapter 4

Chapter 4

The ambush came not with a shout, but with a sigh. It was the sigh of the cypress trees that lined the dusty white road to San Gimignano, a long, whispering exhalation that did not belong to the wind. I had been walking for an hour, the Reverend Fanshawe’s promised visit a cold stone in my stomach, the contessa’s question a persistent, humming note beneath it. The solitude of the road was a relief, a vast, empty chapel after the overcrowded salon of the night before. I was a figure in a landscape, small and blessedly insignificant.

Then the sigh became a rustle, a dry, purposeful scraping against the baked earth. Three figures detached themselves from the deep shadow of a stone wall, their movement not rushed but inexorable, as if they were weeds pushing through cracks in the world’s pavement. They were men of a type I had come to recognise in Florence: lean, faces closed as shuttered windows, eyes that held the flat assessment of a pawnbroker. Their clothes were dusty but not poor. It was their silence that was most terrible. They did not demand my purse. They simply moved to encircle me, their intent a cold, physical pressure in the air.

My own voice, when I found it, was the one I used in the drawing room at Queen’s Gate. “You are mistaken. I have nothing for you.”

The leader, a man with a scar that pulled his lip into a permanent sneer, said nothing. His gaze was fixed on the small velvet reticule I carried, the one containing the fragment of stone I had taken from my aunt’s cabinet. A foolish, impulsive theft, born of that fractured feeling at the party. A piece of her world to hold, to understand. Now it was a beacon.

I took a step back, my heel catching on a rut. The Tennyson in my mind provided nothing but a wash of useless lyricism. This was not poetry. This was the rough prose of the world, and I was illiterate in its language.

The man to my left lunged. His hand was not a fist, but a claw, aimed at the reticule. I twisted away, the green silk of my walking dress tearing on the rough stone of the wall. The sound was shockingly loud, a gasp of protest from the fabric. I felt a hand clamp on my wrist, its grip impersonal and iron-hard. The dread that filled me was not fear of injury, but of violation—the same cold dread of the reverend’s disapproval, made manifest and physical.

Then, a new sound. Not a sigh, not a rustle, but a thrum. It was low, resonant, like a cello string plucked deep in the earth. The air between myself and the grasping man shimmered, not with heat, but with a sudden, viscous density. He recoiled as if stung, shaking his hand.

“Basta.”

The word was not shouted. It was spoken, wearily, as one might speak to a bothersome dog. She emerged from the stand of cypress opposite, a woman of indeterminate age, wrapped in a travel-stained cloak the colour of dried sage. Her hair, a faded bronze streaked with iron grey, was bundled carelessly at her nape. She carried no obvious weapon, only a walking staff of dark, polished wood. Her face was a landscape of its own: fine bones worn sharp by weather and what I could only name as a profound, habitual disappointment. She looked at the men with the tired recognition of one seeing a persistent stain.

The leader recovered, his sneer deepening. “The old woman should walk on. This is no concern of hers.”

“Everything is my concern,” she said, her English precise but accented with the soft vowels of the Italian south. “And you are making a noise in a place that wishes to be quiet. Leave the girl.”

He moved, not toward me, but toward her, swift and serpentine. Her staff moved faster. It did not strike him. She simply planted its end on the road and the world flexed. The air pressure changed, a sudden, painful pop in my ears. The man was thrown backwards, not by a blow, but by the ground itself seeming to rise in a wave against him. He landed in the dust, breathless, his companions frozen in confusion.

She looked at them, her expression one of profound boredom. “The next will break the bone. Yours. Go.”

They went, hauling their leader up, their silence now fuelled by a superstitious terror. They vanished into the landscape as quickly as they had appeared, leaving only the settling dust and the thrumming quiet.

I stood, my back against the sun-warmed stone, the torn silk fluttering at my wrist. My rescuer turned her gaze upon me. It was not kind. It was the assessing, bleak look of a scholar confronted with a badly copied text.

“You are the English girl. The niece.” It was not a question. She sighed, a sound older and deeper than the sigh of the trees. “And you have taken something that does not belong to you. Give it here.”

Shame, hot and immediate, flushed through me. I fumbled with the reticule strings, my fingers clumsy. I drew out the fragment of stone. In the harsh afternoon light, it looked even more unremarkable, a piece of dull, veined rock. Yet as I held it out, I felt it: a faint, answering pulse, like a dormant heartbeat, to the residual energy still humming in the air from her staff.

She took it without touching my skin. Her own fingers, long and stained with what looked like ink or soot, closed around it. She did not look at it. She looked at me.

“You felt that,” she stated. “The resonance.”

“I… I don’t know what I felt.”

“A lie. The first and most useless defence.” She slipped the stone into a pouch at her belt. “Come. They will not return, but this road is no longer safe for you. My cottage is near.”

She did not wait for agreement. She simply turned and began walking up a narrow, overgrown track that branched from the main road. I had no choice but to follow. The confrontation was not over; it had merely changed shape. I had exchanged three rough men for this weary, terrifying woman. I followed, the tear in my dress a tangible mark of the old world’s fragility, the stone’s absence a new, hollow weight in my palm.
  - The cottage was not picturesque. It was a squat, stone structure hunched against a low hill, more a hermitage than a home. Inside, it was a cross between a library and a laboratory. Books were stacked in precarious towers on the floor, their spines cracked and faded. Strange instruments of brass and crystal cluttered a heavy oak table, and the air smelled of dust, dried herbs, and the sharp, metallic scent of ozone.

She gestured to a three-legged stool. “Sit.”

I sat. She busied herself lighting a small spirit lamp under a copper kettle, her movements economical, devoid of wasted motion. She poured water into two chipped earthenware cups, added a pinch of herbs from a jar, and pushed one toward me.

“You may call me Elara,” she said, finally taking the stool opposite. She studied me as she might a problematic equation. “Your aunt moves in her circles of art and gossip. I move in older circles. The circles of foundation and fracture. You have blundered from one into the other.”

“The stone,” I began, my voice too loud in the quiet room. “It was just a curiosity. From my aunt’s collection.”

“Your aunt collects beautiful lies,” Elara said flatly. “Fragments of statues, pieces of pottery. Pretty dead things. What you took is not dead. It is a sleeping danger.” She drew the fragment from her pouch and placed it on the table between us. “This is a piece of a World Seal.”

The term meant nothing to me. It hung in the herb-scented air, heavy and absurd.

She saw my incomprehension. “You think the world is held together by God, or physics, or simple habit. It is more fragile than that. There are… keystones. Seals. Placed in ages so distant they are now myth, to bind the fabric of reality from the erosion of chaos. This,” she tapped the stone with a blunt fingernail, “is a fragment of one such seal. Broken, long ago. By folly. By greed.”

I stared at the unassuming rock. It seemed to mock the grandeur of her words. “It looks like limestone.”

“And a king’s crown looks like gold. Appearance is the least truthful of senses.” She leaned forward. “The breaking was a catastrophe contained. The fragments were scattered, hidden, their power dormant. But power, like water, seeks its own level. It can be sensed. It can be called. There are those who seek to gather the fragments. Not to repair. To unmake.”

“Unmake what?”

“Everything.” She said it without drama. It was a simple, dreadful fact. “A gathered set of fragments, activated by the correct ritual, would not rebuild the seal. It would become a key. A key to turn the lock on the door of the world, and open it to the void that lies behind all things. Your ambush today was not for your purse. It was for this. They are seekers. They have other pieces. They felt the awakening resonance of this one the moment you, in your ignorance, formed an attachment to it.”

The tea was bitter on my tongue. The cottage walls seemed to press closer. I thought of the party, of Leo’s wink, the contessa’s probing question, the reverend’s cold concern. They were all part of a parlor game. This was something else entirely. This was the tapestry of the world, and I had pulled a thread without knowing I was holding a needle.

“Why me?” The question was a whisper.
“Why not you?” Elara countered. “Chaos is not selective. But there is a reason you felt its pulse. A reason they targeted you and not your aunt, who has owned it for years. The power within the fragment resonates with a latent potential within you. A potential you have been taught, in your drawing-room world, to ignore, to suppress, to call nerves or imagination.”

The primavera truth of the night before echoed in her words. Freedom was a confrontation. This was a confrontation with the very nature of myself.

“What must I do?”

“First,” she said, finishing her tea, “you must learn to see. Not with your eyes.” She rose and cleared a space on the cluttered floor. “Stand here.”

Warily, I obeyed. She placed the fragment of the World Seal on the floor before me.

“Close your eyes. Breathe. And tell me what you feel. Not what you think. What you feel.”

I closed my eyes. The darkness was alive with the after-images of the sun. I heard the hiss of the lamp, the dry rustle of a mouse in the thatch. I felt foolish.

“Concentrate. Stop listening to the room. Listen to the space between the sounds.”

I tried. I let the sounds recede. I focused on the hollow space the stone had left in my awareness. Slowly, like a faint star appearing as one gazes at the night sky, I felt it. Not a sight, not a sound. A presence. A point of subtle gravitational pull, a tiny, dense knot of… otherness. It was cold and ancient and impossibly still, yet at its core, it vibrated with a captive energy.

“It’s… cold,” I murmured. “A deep cold. And there’s a hum. A held note.”
“Good.” Her voice was closer now. “Now, in your mind, reach for it. Not with a hand. With your attention. Greet it. Acknowledge its presence.”

I imagined my awareness as a tendril, extending from the centre of my mind. I touched the edge of that cold knot. The hum intensified, not in the room, but in the bones of my skull. A faint, silvery light seemed to glow behind my eyelids.

“It… responds.”
“It recognises a like frequency,” Elara said. Her voice held no praise, only clinical observation. “Now, the difficult part. You must not be drawn into its stillness. You must impose your own rhythm upon it. Gently. Imagine your breath is a tide. And with each exhalation, you push a wave of that tide toward the stone, warming its edges.”

I breathed in, then out, slowly. With the exhale, I pictured the warmth of my own life, the pulse of my blood, flowing toward that cold point. For several breaths, nothing happened. Then, I felt the slightest yielding. The cold did not diminish, but a thread of connection formed, a filament of shared vibration between my own core and the stone’s dormant power. A bead of sweat traced a path down my spine. The effort was not physical, but profound, a straining of a mental muscle I had never known I possessed.

“Enough.” Her hand was on my shoulder, grounding me. I opened my eyes, blinking in the lamplight. The world seemed sharper, louder, more vividly coloured. I felt exhausted and exhilarated, as after a long, hard ride.

“You have taken the first step,” she said. “You have sensed and touched. Control will come later, with practice you do not have time for. The seekers are moving. We must move faster.”
  - For three days, I lived in the suspended world of Elara’s cottage. I told my aunt, via a carefully worded note delivered by a paid boy from the village, that I was taking a private retreat at a convent after the distress of the road. It was a lie she would accept; it fit the narrative of a fragile English sensibility. The truth was my education in fragility had only just begun.

Elara was a relentless, impatient tutor. We practised for hours each day. She taught me to expand my sense, to feel not just the roaring flame of the World Seal fragment, but the smaller energies around us: the slow, deep song of the hill beneath the cottage, the quick, bright sparks of life in the field mice in the walls, the whispering network of roots in the earth. My world, once composed of objects and social cues, became a symphony of vibrating forces. It was beautiful and terrifying.

“The power is not in the fragment itself,” she explained one evening as we sat amidst the clutter. “The fragment is a lens. A focusing crystal. The power is in the fabric of existence. The Seal was a dam, holding back a pressure. The broken pieces can still tap that pressure, can focus it into a tool… or a weapon. You have a capacity to be a lens as well. A weaker, organic one. That is why they want you. A living lens is far more malleable than stone.”

On the fourth evening, as a soft rain pattered against the small window, the strain of her relentless instruction seemed to crack her stern composure. We were reviewing a complex diagram of energy channels—her own life’s work, sketched in a battered journal. Her finger traced a branching path that ended abruptly in a blot of ink, as if the pen had been slammed down.

“I was not always alone in this watch,” she said, her voice softer than I had ever heard it. It was the voice of the woman she might have been, before the weight of knowledge settled on her. “I had a teacher. And I had a… a fellow student. His name was Kaelen.”

She fell silent for so long I thought she would not continue. The rain filled the quiet.

“He was brilliant. A natural lens, as you are. But where you are cautious, he was ravenous. He saw not duty in our work, but opportunity. Power.” She closed the journal, her hand resting on the cover as if on a tombstone. “We discovered a fragment, larger than this, hidden in a tomb on Cyprus. The moment he touched it, I felt the change. It was not a resonance. It was a marriage. He believed the legends of the Seals were lies told by the fearful. He believed the fragments could be used not to mend, but to remake the world according to a… a clearer vision. His vision.”

I could see it in her eyes, the memory playing out in the dim light. “What happened?”

“I tried to stop him. We fought. Not with fists, but with the very currents we had learned to wield. It was a ugly, tearing thing. I was stronger in technique, but he was stronger in will, fuelled by the fragment’s power. In the end, to prevent him from taking it, I was forced to use a dispersion ritual. It shattered the fragment into a dozen smaller pieces. It also… it unmade the bonds of energy holding the tomb’s ceiling aloft.”

She looked directly at me, her weariness now absolute. “He was buried. And the fragments, including the one I had just created, were scattered to the winds. My failure was twofold: I did not save my friend from his own hunger, and I turned one grave problem into a dozen scattered ones. The seekers you met are his disciples, or the disciples of those who found his notes. They continue his work. They have gathered some of the pieces. This,” she nodded toward the stone on the table, “is one of the last they need.”

Her confession hung in the room. This was not a grand battle of good and evil from a storybook. It was a tragedy of arrogance and flawed love, and its consequences were still unspooling. Her sternness, her bleakness, were not innate. They were the scars.

“You hope to stop them. To redeem your mistake,” I said quietly.

“Redemption is a fairy tale for your English poets,” she said, but the bitterness was gone, replaced by a simple, stark honesty. “I hope to prevent the catastrophe I helped set in motion. That is all. And now, I hope that you…” She trailed off, looking at me not as a tool, but as a person, for the first time. “I hope you are stronger than I was. Not in power. In character. The world does not need another brilliant lens. It needs a steady one.”
  - The next morning, she woke me before dawn. “They are close. I can feel the search-pulse, like a drumbeat in the deep earth. You must go.”

“Go? Where? I can’t just leave you.”
“You must. You are the variable they cannot account for. They know of me. They expect me. They do not fully understand you.” She pressed a small, wrapped bundle into my hands. It was the World Seal fragment. “Take it. Hide it in plain sight. Return to your aunt’s world. The glittering salons are as good a hiding place as any. Better, perhaps. Learn to control your gift there, amidst the noise. It will be the hardest practice of all.”

“But what will you do?”
“I will lead them a merry chase. I am good at that.” A ghost of a smile touched her lips, there and gone. “When it is time, I will find you. Remember your training. Sense the world. Feel for the dissonance of their seeking. Trust the primavera in you, the green, growing truth, not the dead stone.”

She ushered me out into the pre-dawn grey. The air was cool and damp. She gave me directions to a different path, one that would loop me back to Florence by a circuitous route.

I stood on the threshold, the fragment heavy in my bundle, the weight of her story and my own nascent power a mantle on my shoulders. I was no longer a ghost between two worlds. I was a secret in one, a weapon in the making for the other.

“Thank you, Elara.”
“Do not thank me. I have burdened you with the fate I failed to carry.” She placed a hand, briefly, on my head. It was a benediction and an apology. “Now go. And when the Reverend calls, listen to his small fears. They will remind you of the scale of the true ones.”

I turned and walked into the grey light. I did not look back. When I had gone a hundred yards, I opened my senses, as she had taught me. I felt the deep, slow song of the hill, the sleeping trees, the scurry of a fox. And behind me, from the cottage, I felt Elara’s own energy, a complex, sorrowful melody of resolve and regret. Then, with a deliberate, wrenching focus, she amplified it, sending out a clarion call of power into the waking world—a beacon to draw the hunters to her, and away from me.

I understood then the final, terrible lesson. Sacrifice was not a moment. It was a path one walked, every day. I clutched the bundle tighter and walked on, toward the city of beautiful lies, carrying a fragment of an ugly truth. The confrontation was not beginning. It had already been joined, generations ago, and I was now a living thread in its fraying tapestry. And I knew, with a cold certainty, that the next drumbeat I felt in the deep earth would not be theirs searching for the stone.

It would be mine.



---

Chapter 5

Chapter 5

The drumbeat began a mile outside the city walls. It was not the deep, searching thrum of the hunters’ stone. It was my own, as I had known it would be. A percussive pulse in the soles of my feet, a counter-rhythm to my own frantic heart. It was not a call. It was a warning.

Elara’s beacon had done its work, drawing their attention like iron filings to a lodestone. I had felt the violent, discordant rip of their power tearing through the hills toward her cottage, a soundless shriek in the fabric of the world. I had not stopped walking. My path, as she had intended, was clear of them. But she had underestimated their numbers, or their cunning. A second thread of pursuit, thinner, more subtle, had been spun out toward Florence. I felt it now—a cold, greasy sensation against the nape of my neck, a wrongness in the taste of the morning air. They were not close, but they were patient. They had the scent.

The city, when I reached it, was no longer a haven of beautiful lies. It was a painted trap. Every shadow in the narrow vicoli seemed to hold a watching stillness. The cry of a market vendor was a coded signal; the clatter of a cart over cobbles, the closing of a door. My training, so new and untested, hummed beneath my skin. I did not see the world, I sensed it: the weary, warm murmur of the baker’s oven, the sharp, anxious tang of a merchant counting his lire, the slow, damp decay of stone in a shaded alley. And threading through it all, the dissonance. A patch of silence where there should be sound. A chill where the sun fell. A figure in a dark cloak whose presence left no ripple in the human current around him, a stone in a stream that caused no eddy.

I moved not with a ghost’s uncertainty, but with a predator’s wariness. I became a part of the city’s own discord—a student late for lectures, cutting through the Piazza della Signoria with my head down, my bundle of books and the fragment within it clutched to my chest. The dissonance followed, a half-beat behind my own movements.

Elara’s directions were a map etched in panic and necessity. Not to my lodgings. Not to the Reverend’s quiet study with its small fears. She had sent me to the one place in Florence that did not belong to the Renaissance, nor to the modern age. The Gates, she had whispered with her last breath of instruction, were not of stone and mortar. They were a forgetting. A place the world had turned its back upon.

I found it behind the church of Santa Croce, in a courtyard so forgotten it was little more than a widening in the alley, choked with bindweed and the skeletons of old lemon trees. A high wall, blotched with lichen, held a recessed archway. The iron gate within it was rusted shut, its bars a lattice for ivy so thick it seemed the vegetation was the true gate, the metal its ghost. This was it. Not a grand arch, but a wound in the city’s side, scabbed over with green.

The dissonance sharpened behind me. It was at the mouth of the alley now. A pressure, a silence that ate the distant sounds of the city. I turned.

There were two of them. They wore the dark, serviceable clothes of scholars or clerks, but they wore them as a uniform. Their faces were not cruel, but empty, as if the person within had been scooped out and replaced with a single, focused will. I recognized the type. It was the same careful neutrality Mrs. Briggs had worn the day the letter came, but here it was not arranged; it was absolute.

One of them, a man with greying hair and eyes the colour of a winter puddle, spoke. His voice was pleasant, devoid of threat. It was the most threatening thing I had ever heard.

“Alexandra. Your aunt is concerned. She wishes you to come home.”

The use of my full name, the invocation of Aunt Maud—the small, domestic fear—was a masterstroke. It was a hook meant to snag the part of me that still lived in Queen’s Gate, that still jumped at the chime of a teacup. For a second, the drumbeat in my feet faltered.

Then I felt it. Not from them, but from the bundle in my arms. A warmth, a single, clear note that vibrated through the cloth and into my bones. The primavera. The green, growing truth. It cut through the greasy dissonance of their presence like a shaft of sunlight through oil-slick water.

“I am not going back,” I said. My voice did not sound like my own. It was lower, steadier, woven through with the new thread of power I carried.

The second hunter, a younger woman with a placid, unmemorable face, tilted her head. “The item you carry does not belong to you. It is a danger. You are not equipped to understand its nature.”

The dead stone. They spoke of it as an object, a thing. They could not feel the life in it, the memory. Their power was one of acquisition, of containment. Of silencing.

“I understand enough,” I said. I took a step backward, toward the ivy-choked gate.

The man’s pleasant expression did not change. “There is no need for unpleasantness. We are here to help.”

He moved. It was not a run, but a swift, gliding advance, horribly efficient. The woman moved to flank me. The air around them grew colder, the dissonance swelling to a deafening silent roar. It was a pressure meant to paralyse, to crush the will.

I did not think. I reached.

Not out, but in. To the fragment in my bundle, and to the deep, slow song of the earth beneath the city’s thin skin. I felt the ancient hill upon which Florence was built, the sleeping river, the roots of the bindweed that choked the courtyard. And I asked.

The response was not gentle. It was a surge of raw, verdant force, a torrent that rushed up from the ground and through me. It was not my power. I was merely its conduit, its momentary shape. The ivy on the gate erupted. Not with growth, but with violent, sentient motion. Thick tendrils, old as ropes, lashed out from the wall like green whips. They snapped around the ankles of the advancing man, yanking him off his feet. The bindweed on the ground writhed, tangling around the woman’s legs, climbing with terrifying speed.

Their dissonant power flared, a sickly, grey light that withered the leaves where it touched, turning them black and brittle. But for every vine they killed, two more surged from the stone, from the very dirt. The courtyard was no longer a forgotten corner. It was a waking, furious entity.

I turned my back on their struggle, the sounds of tearing vegetation and muffled curses. The gate. I had to open the gate.

Elara had said it required a key of energy, a specific resonance. The fragment was the key. But how to turn it? I placed my hands on the rusted iron, feeling the cold, dead metal beneath the living veil of ivy. I pushed my awareness into it, seeking a mechanism, a lock.

Nothing. Only age and decay.

The drumbeat in my feet became a frantic tattoo. The hunters’ dissonance was reasserting itself, burning through the green defense. I could feel their fury now, a hot, focused point of malice. The woman had almost freed herself.

Sense the world, Elara’s voice echoed in my memory. Feel for the dissonance.

I closed my eyes. I stopped trying to see the gate. I felt for what was wrong here, what was out of tune. Not the hunters’ presence, but the gate’s own nature. And I found it. The archway was not just forgotten. It was unmade. A memory the world had tried to erase. The dissonance was in the stone itself—a hum of wrongness, of a song cut off mid-bar.

The fragment in my bundle sang its single, clear note in response.

I did not try to force the gate open. I tried to remember it open.

I poured the fragment’s energy, filtered through my own desperate will, into that hum of wrongness. I did not seek to fix it, but to complete it. To sing the rest of the barred song. The note from the fragment swelled, joined by the deeper chord of the hill beneath me. I felt a vibration in the air, a tuning fork struck against the bone of the world.

The rusted iron gate did not move. It shimmered. The solid metal became insubstantial, not as mist, but as a memory of itself. Through it, I no longer saw the wall of the next courtyard. I saw a blur of green and gold and impossible light. I felt a wind from another sky, warm and smelling of ozone and crushed herbs.

A cry of rage from behind me. The male hunter had torn free, a blade of grey energy in his hand. He lunged.

I did not step through the gate. I fell forward into the shimmer, into the song.
  - The world tore.

It was not a passage, but an unmaking. The solidity of stone, the weight of history, the very concept of up and down—all of it unraveled into a roaring kaleidoscope of sensation. I was a thread pulled through the eye of a needle made of light and thunder. I felt the fragment scream in harmony with the passage, a shrill, sustaining note that held the fraying edges of my being together.

Then, silence.

A silence so profound it was itself a sound. I lay on my back, the breath knocked from my lungs. The bundle was still in my arms. I was whole.

I opened my eyes.

The sky was not blue. It was a vast, pearlescent dome the colour of a dove’s breast, shot through with slow, drifting veils of violet and copper. There were two suns. One was a familiar, golden disc, low on the horizon. The other, smaller and cooler, was a silver coin hanging higher up, casting a second, fainter shadow. The light was alien, ethereal, painting the world in hues of amber and lavender.

I pushed myself up. I was in a meadow, but the grass was not green. It was a silvery-blue, each blade tipped with a minute, phosphorescent pearl that glowed softly in the dual light. Flowers like blown glass, in shapes I had no names for, trembled on stems of crystalline fibre. The air was indeed warm, thick with the scent of ozone and a sweet, spicy perfume I could not identify. It was breathable, but it felt more than air—it felt alive, charged, as if each inhalation was a drink of something vital.

I turned. The gate was gone. Behind me was a standing stone, twice my height, covered in the same silvery moss that clung to the trees at the edge of the meadow. It was inert. Just a rock. The way back was closed.

The finality of it was a physical blow. Queen’s Gate, Florence, Elara, the hunters—all of it was gone. Not just distant, but other. A page turned in a book I could not reopen. I was not between worlds anymore. I was in one. And I had left the other behind, definitively.

The generational weight of it settled upon me. My mother’s quiet fear, my father’s oblivious rustling of the newspaper, Aunt Maud’s manipulative concern—they were not just personal history. They were the symptoms of a world that had forgotten this one, that had sealed the gates and called it safety. Elara’s burden, her failure, her sacrifice—they were the legacy of those who remembered, and were broken by the remembering. I was the next in that line. Not a ghost, not a secret. An heir. And the inheritance was this impossible sky, this singing grass, and the fragment of a power that had shattered a civilization.

I stood, my legs unsteady. The disorientation was not just of place, but of self. The rules were different here. I could feel it in my blood, a buzzing, effervescent potential. I took a tentative step. The glowing grass beneath my foot released a cloud of tiny, luminous spores that drifted around my ankle. I felt a jolt, not unpleasant, like the static charge from a carpet.

I reached out with my senses, as Elara had taught me. In Florence, it had been like listening through wool. Here, it was like removing a deafening hood.

The world sang.

The song was not music, but a layered, complex tapestry of living vibration. The deep, slow pulse of the earth was a bass note, richer and more resonant than anything I had felt before. The trees at the meadow’s edge hummed with a verdant chorus, each leaf a tiny resonator. The very light seemed to have a frequency, a gentle, golden hum. And I could feel the fragment’s melody woven into it all, no longer a solitary note, but a thread in a grand, ancient harmony.

I felt a pull, a faint but insistent tugging in my chest, like a compass needle finding north. It was the fragment, resonating with something in this world. Something distant, but kin.

I began to walk, following the pull. My body felt strange. Lighter. Each movement seemed to require less effort, as if the air itself offered a subtle buoyancy. I passed under the canopy of the trees. They were not oaks or pines. Their trunks were smooth and grey like elephant skin, branching high above into great, feathery fronds of copper and bronze. As I walked beneath them, the fronds trembled, releasing a soft, chiming sound, like distant bells.

I came to a stream. The water was clear, but it flowed with a viscous, syrupy slowness, and it glittered with suspended motes of light. I knelt to drink, cupping my hands. The water was cool and sweet, and as it touched my lips, a shock of clarity went through me. My senses, already heightened, sharpened further. I could see the individual light-motes in the air, could track the path of a tiny, six-winged insect as it zipped between flowers. I could hear the sap moving in the tree beside me, a slow, rhythmic rush.

The pull grew stronger. I crossed the stream, my boots sinking into the soft, luminous bank on the other side. As I climbed a gentle rise, I saw it.

In the valley below, under the twin suns, lay the ruins of Aetheria.

It was not a city as I understood the word. There were no straight lines, no right angles. It was a sprawling, organic cascade of structures that seemed to have grown from the hill itself, not built upon it. Towers curved like unfurling ferns, their surfaces shimmering with a mother-of-pearl iridescence. Bridges of woven, living vine spanned gaps between structures. Great domes, cracked and open to the sky, were lined with crystalline lattices that caught the light and fractured it into rainbows. It was breathtaking, a symphony in stone and crystal and petrified wood. And it was utterly, profoundly dead.

The song here was different. A haunting, minor-key echo of the vibrant life in the meadow. The deep pulse of the earth was muted beneath the ruins, as if the land itself was grieving. The pull from the fragment became a keen, a mournful yearning.

This was the source. The cradle of the power I carried. The place of the Shattering.

I made my way down into the valley, moving through streets that were not streets, but gentle, spiraling pathways. The silence was a presence. No bird sang here. No insects hummed. The only sound was the whisper of the strange wind through the broken crystal lattices, a sound like a thousand distant wind chimes.

I reached a central plaza, a vast, open space paved with seamless, opalescent stone. In its centre stood a dais, and upon the dais, the shattered stump of what must have been a colossal tree, its petrified remains twisted in a final, agonized contortion. This was the heart. I could feel it.

I climbed the dais, drawn irresistibly. As I reached the petrified trunk, the fragment in my bundle grew hot. I unwrapped it. The grey stone, so dull in my world, was glowing with a soft, internal light, its veins of green pulsing like a heartbeat.

I placed my hand on the cold, dead wood of the ancient tree.

The vision did not come as a memory. It came as an invasion.

I was not myself. I was a thousand selves. I was the primavera, the world-song, given conscious form. I felt the deep, joyous connection to every root, every leaf, every stream and stone in a thriving, pulsing world. I felt the harmony of it, a civilization that did not build upon the land, but with it, their art and their magic and their lives an extension of the natural chord.

Then, the dissonance. Not from outside. From within. A faction. They heard the song not as a thing to join, but as a force to be directed. They sought to isolate the notes, to amplify some and silence others, to compose a new, more “perfect” song. They crafted the first dead stones—not fragments of the living whole, but cages for its power. Tools. The conflict was not a war of weapons, but of philosophies. A schism in the choir. The generational conflict was here, at the very beginning. It was not my family against Aunt Maud. It was the idea of harmony against the will to control.

I felt the moment of the Shattering. Not an explosion, but a terrible, silent unraveling. As the controllers tried to seize the core of the world-song, the great tree at the heart of Aetheria, the harmony rebelled. The song tore itself apart rather than be enslaved. The tree petrified in an instant. The city died. The gates to other worlds, like the one I had passed through, snapped shut or were forgotten. The controllers, those who survived, were cast out, their dead stones the only remnants of their ambition. They became the hunters. And the primavera was shattered into fragments, scattered across worlds.

The vision released me. I stumbled back from the petrified trunk, gasping, my face wet with tears I did not remember shedding. I understood everything now. The conflict was not about possessing a relic. It was about the nature of power itself. The hunters sought to finish what their ancestors had started: to gather the fragments, force them back into a whole, and control it. Elara’s line, and now I, sought to protect the fragments, to keep the song alive in its broken, diffuse state. To prevent a second, final silencing.

I was not just a carrier of a fragment. I was a carrier of the original sin, and the original resistance.

As the echoes of the vision faded, I became aware of a new sensation. The air around me was stirring. The motes of light were gathering, swirling. The faint, mournful song of the ruins was changing pitch, quickening. The fragment in my hand blazed brighter.

They were coming.

Not hunters from my world. Something else. The vision had been a trigger. My presence, my fragment, had sounded a note in this dead place, and something had heard it.

From the archways of the broken towers, from the shadows of the petrified tree, they emerged. Figures of light and shadow, vaguely humanoid but fluid, as if made of condensed memory and regret. The ghosts of Aetheria. The echo of the choir that was lost. They did not advance with menace, but with a terrible, slow curiosity. They were the silence after the song, and they were drawn to the last, faint echo of it in my hand.

I stood on the dais, the heir to a shattered legacy, in a dead city under an alien sky, facing the ghosts of the very conflict I now embodied. The fragment’s light painted my face in hues of green and pearl. The drumbeat in my feet was gone. In its place was the whole, terrible, beautiful symphony of a murdered world, and I was the only one left to hear it.

I raised the fragment, not as a weapon, but as an answer. A single, clear note in the silence.

The ghosts paused. Then, as one, they began to move toward me, and the sound of their movement was the sound of a thousand pages turning, of a forgotten language being spoken for the first time in millennia.

The confrontation had not begun. It had been waiting for me.



---

Chapter 6

Chapter 6

The ghosts did not advance with the rustle of robes or the shuffle of feet, but with the sigh of settling dust. It was the sound of the city itself drawing a breath it had forgotten how to exhale. The fragment in my hand was no longer a mere shard of crystal; it was a lodestone, and these phantoms of condensed memory were iron filings pulled from the ruins of their own history.

I held my ground on the dais, the raised stone cold and certain beneath my boots. The instinct to flee was a sharp, animal cry in my blood, but the weight of the vision—the crushing, beautiful burden of it—anchored me. These were not hunters. They were the aftermath. The silence after the symphony. To run would be to dishonor the very song I now carried.

They encircled the dais, a nebulous corona of faint light and deeper shadow. Features swam within their forms—a suggestion of an eye, the echo of a hand—but nothing held. They were like faces seen in the grain of old wood, present only so long as you did not look directly. Their collective gaze, however, was a tangible pressure. It was not hostile. It was hungry. It was the hunger of a library for a reader, of a instrument for a hand to play it.

One, slightly more defined than the others, drifted closer. Where its feet should have touched the petrified moss, a faint, phosphorescent pattern bloomed and died.

“You hear it,” the ghost said, though its voice did not come from it. It came from the air between us, from the vibration in the fragment, from the back of my own mind. It was the voice of the ruins given words. “You hold the echo.”

“I hold a fragment,” I replied, and my own voice sounded coarse, blunt, a stone dropped into still water. “I have seen… I have seen the choir. The fall.”

A ripple passed through the gathered forms, a shimmer of anguish and recognition. The defining one seemed to solidify a degree more. I could now make out the suggestion of robes, of a tall, slender frame.

“Seeing is a weight,” the ghost intoned. “To remember is to carry. Why do you come to this place of carrying, heir of the shatter?”

The question was not an accusation. It was a test of resonance, like a tuning fork struck against a stone.

“I am being hunted,” I said, the truth simple and stark. “For what I carry. I was told… I was told I might find answers here. A way to understand what I am supposed to do.”

“Answers are not given,” the chorus of whispers sighed. “They are remembered. You have remembered the fall. Now you must learn the consequence.”

The defining ghost raised its insubstantial arm. The swirling motes of light in the air coalesced, drawn into a vortex above its palm. Within the vortex, images flickered—not the grand, terrible tapestry of my vision, but smaller, sharper scenes. A figure, human but glowing with an inner light, hiding a shard in the hollow of a great tree. Another, face etched with desperation, sealing a fragment within a river stone. A third, weeping, burying a piece of crystal in soft earth as boots thundered in the distance.

“The Scattering,” I whispered.

“The preservation,” the ghost corrected. “The first act of the resistance. Not to fight the hunters, but to deny them the whole. To seed the world with the echo, so that the song could never be truly silenced. Your bloodline is the thread that ties the seeds to the earth. You are the gardener of the broken song.”

The weight settled deeper, a mantle of responsibility that was both exquisite and terrifying. I was not a soldier. I was a custodian. A tender of sacred, scattered things.

“The hunters have returned,” I said. “They seek to gather the fragments again. To finish it.”

The light in the ghost’s hand snuffed out. The silence that followed was profound, a held breath that stretched across centuries.

“Then you must not be found,” the whispers said, and for the first time, a note of urgency, of fear, threaded through them. “The path you walk is narrow, between the greed that would cage the song and the despair that would let it fade. This place is a tomb. It is also a crossroads. You must go to the living wood, to the Verdant Maze. There, the oldest guardians remember the pact.”

“Guardians?”

But the ghosts were already dissolving, their forms streaming away like smoke caught in a sudden draft. The defining one lingered a moment longer. It reached out, and though its hand had no substance, a sensation like cool mist brushed my forehead.

“Remember the weight,” it breathed, its voice fading into the eternal sigh of the ruins. “And do not trust the silence.”

Then they were gone. The motes of light returned to their idle dance. The mournful song of the wind through the towers was just the wind once more. I was alone, the fragment’s light dimming to a soft, internal pulse. The confrontation had not been a battle. It had been an inheritance. And a warning.

The way out of the dead city was shown to me not by signs, but by a subtle pull in the fragment itself, a faint, magnetic tug toward the western edge of the ruins. It led me through a shattered aqueduct and down a slope overgrown with a strange, silver-grey moss that whispered underfoot. By the time the last spire of Aetheria vanished behind the evergreen curtain of the forest, the sky had deepened to the color of a bruise. I had exchanged a city of ghosts for a wood that felt intensely, watchfully alive.

This was the Verdant Maze.

The trees here were not the friendly, dappled sentinels of the woods near home. They were ancient, gnarled titans, their trunks thick with moss and strange, bioluminescent fungi that glowed with a sickly greenish pallor. Vines hung like slumbering serpents, and the air was thick with the scent of damp earth, rotting blossoms, and something else—something sharp and metallic, like ozone after a lightning strike. It was the smell of active, untamed magic.

Elara had spoken of this place in hushed tones. A living barrier, she called it, grown by the first guardians in the aftermath of the Scattering to protect certain hidden places. It did not welcome visitors.

I had taken perhaps two dozen steps under the oppressive canopy when the Maze introduced itself. The path before me, a mere suggestion in the undergrowth, simply… shifted. A great root, previously submerged in loam, rose up like the spine of a waking beast. The whispering moss to my right retreated, revealing a patch of ground that shimmered with a deceptive, oily beauty. I felt the fragment at my chest give a distinct, warning thrum.

This was the test. Not of strength, but of perception. Of listening.

I moved forward, not with the blind haste of fear, but with the deliberate care my mother had used when examining a fragile, precious artifact. I let the fragment’s subtle vibrations guide me. A low pulse meant danger close. A quick, fluttering sensation suggested a path, a possibility. I learned to read the language of the forest itself—the way a particular cluster of glowing fungi would dim as I passed, the way a specific vine would twitch, not from wind, but from my proximity.

My new skills, whispered to me by the fragment and honed by the ghostly inheritance, were not for combat. They were for harmony. Or at least, for non-aggression. I found I could extend a thread of the fragment’s energy, a mere filament of that ancient song, and brush it against the consciousness of the Maze. It was not communication. It was a presentation of credentials. I carry a piece of the thing you were made to guard. I am not the enemy.

Once, a magnificent, stag-like creature with bark-like skin and antlers of woven thorn stepped onto the path ahead. Its eyes were pools of liquid shadow. It lowered its head, not in threat, but in inquiry. I stood very still, letting the fragment’s light seep through my tunic. I hummed, low in my throat, a note that was not a melody but an acknowledgment of the silence the ghosts had warned me of. The creature watched me for a long moment, then snorted a puff of spore-laden air and melted back into the gloom.

The Maze was a relentless tutor. It taught me patience where I had none, and observation where I had only anxiety. Hours bled together under the eternal twilight of the canopy. I was parched, my clothes snagged and stained, but the gnawing dread had been replaced by a focused, humming alertness. I was learning the grammar of this world.

It was this hard-won alertness that saved me when the shriek tore through the wood.

It was a sound of pure, predatory fury, followed by a frantic, human shout. The fragment jolted against my sternum, a spike of alarm. I moved toward the sound, not recklessly, but using the Maze’s own tricks—stepping where the roots were quiet, keeping my silhouette broken by the giant ferns.

I came to the edge of a small, fungus-choked clearing. In its center, a young man was backed against the trunk of a massive, pulsating toadstool. He was dressed in hides and leathers reinforced with what looked like carved bone, his skin tanned and marked with intricate green tattoos that swirled up his arms.
